RATING: 2
When you see this game for the first time, you wanna play immediately. Colorful, with great card design...

But then you start playing, you notice how many issues there are with this game :
- First : it's boring. You have fun when it's your turn and you're combotting like crazy. But then it's the others 3 players turn and you have nothing to do except watching them play. The fact that 90% of what they do has no impact on your game makes it even more boring. Just keep track of the 1 or 2 cards that can affect you, and that's all. 
Race makes you play all together to avoid this, Dominion forces you to check the others's actions. Both are fun to play. Seasons does neither of theses things, and is boring.
- Second : It's a game where you can be virtually eliminated, but still be forced to play until the end. When you are losing, chances are that every thing will be even more difficult than you, so you will keep losing. You know you have no way to get back in the game, but the game won't let you go.
- Finally, I'm a bit worried by some of the combinations. Contrary to Race you can play the same card twice. If you happens to get the same "Do this to get X points" card twice (there are 2 of each card), you can easily rack up points by doing the same thing over and over.
There is a reason why Race prevents you from playing the same card twice : it can be abused too easily.

This comment is based on 3 plays (with 2, 3, and 4 players). I was worried after my first play, but I wanted to still give a chance to this game. After playing again and seeing the same issues twice I say stop, it's too much. I tried, but for me the game is broken
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
The greatest sorcerers of the kingdom have gathered at the heart of the Argos forest, where the legendary tournament of the 12 seasons is taking place. At the end of the three year competition, the new archmage of the kingdom of Xidit will be chosen from among the competitors. Take your place, wizard! Equip your ancestral magical items, summon your most faithful familiars to your side and be ready to face the challenge!

Each player can choose only one die per turn. The die not chosen by anyone determines how many fields the "time track" would move forward.

At the end of the game, the crystals are summed with victory points granted by the cards (minus some penalties, where applicable). The highest score wins.

Great as a 2 player, or 3 player. 4 and up is way to long.

RATING: 6
Played several times, 2p, 3p, 4p; beginner version.  Not as good as I had hoped.  This is an extremely tactical game.  In general, you can not plan for what your opponents are going to do, so you must simply make as many points on your turn as you can.  It is fairly easy to stumble into a killer card combo, but, on the other hand, it is also fairly easy to get stuck with non-complementing cards too.  I wonder if the more advanced versions feel more strategic, but they will suffer the fairly common problem of familiarity with the cards will produce a considerable advantage.  The jury is very much still out on this one, but I have not enjoyed the game thus far.

RATING: 3
I think the first complaint that everybody said about this game is that some cards are too powerful. It can be true, but if all cards are too powerful, then it does not unbalance the game. Personally, it's not what bothers me most. The worst situation is when a player could play a card from the deck for free and managed to play a 30 point VP card. That simply gave him the game.

The card drafting at the beginning of the game is the most annoying mechanic. How am I going to know the cards I need to have and when I am going to need them. It also slow down dramatically the game at the beginning and it's not fun. Most of the time, I selected my cards randomly. 

Personally, I would have distributed cards randomly, but if you really want to keep the drafting, why not make a 3 card draft at the beginning of each year. It would make much more sense since being in the game, you now know what you need.

Crystallization is also a bit useless, not only it does not give a lot of crystals, but there are cards that gives much more crystals than flushing power. Second, you need to have a die with a circle to be able to crystallize, which makes the action even less attractive. I think I have not crystallized even once through the whole game.

Finally, the lack of first player maker makes it very confusing to keep track of who started to play and some times a player almost had made a double turn. There is also a lot of down time because you need to wait for other players to do their stuff. I managed to accelerate the game a bit by collecting your resources while the others are selecting their dice. Only card play could not be done simultaneously.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Mechanics are simple, but it is difficult to know which cards are good the first time you play. Despite dice rolls the drafting mechanic of the dice and cards makes this game's luck not a huge deal; the only huge concern of luck is when you are top decking cards due to other card effects. Lots of cool combos that you can perform, and I feel like all their designs were well thought out; almost every card seems powerful with the right cards to complement them. Game play and viability of certain cards also is really different for different number of players. Interestingly, this game shares a lot of things I don't like about Settlers (decisions early game that can totally mess you up for the rest of the game, not being able to do much for many of your turns), yet my ratings for the two are very different.

The pips of the leftover die may not seem to be very important your first few plays, but managing the clock is extremely important. In fact, at times it can be so powerful that some consider Temporal Boots to be one of the best cards in the game (in fact, I would suggest telling new players that such an item exists so they don't get screwed over by it their first time).

EDIT: After 20 plays this game appears to be a lot less predetermined following the card drafting than I initially thought. Taking the risky draw a card can very drastically affect how you play the rest of the game. At the same time being efficient with having the right energy at the right time can score you a bunch of residual points, which between skilled player can be the difference between victory and defeat. Furthermore, most of the single use cards require good timing to get maximum value. However, this game has taken a lot more effort to shine as I had initially expected, as the initial game without drafting is a lot less fun to me than the future games with. Even then, I prefer playing with the advanced cards too, and so there is an effective 2 game investment when teaching new players.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The heart of the game is that you have 9 cards (we played the preset beginner decks) that you split into 3, representing the cards you will have to play in each of the 3 years that the game spans. You gather energy tokens (4 colors, each related to a season) and increase your ability to summon more cards, all the time trying to score crystals, which are the VP but are also a resource of their own. On your turn, you roll dice according to the current season then each player gets to pick a die, giving them resources. Then you spend them as you are able, to play cards or score points. Hard to grasp at first, it was quite smooth after a couple of turns. Managing your resources is a big part but the card combos - playing them down, getting them to work together - is what will bring players back to this game. I am predisposed to liking this game as card combos and special power card games are right in my wheelhouse, but it was really engaging. Will keep an eye out for another game of this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
In Seasons, you roll dice to get "manna" to place cards.  You also try to advance on a track that allows you to play more cards.  There is a simple draft on 9 cards that come out three at a time over three years.  The art on the cards is great and the game reminds me of a lighter Magic the Gathering.  

There is a lot of player interaction, however, there may be too much.  The game becomes fiddly because you must remember when some event occurs you or your opponents may gain or lose points based on cards they have played.

This game ought to be family weight but due to the card text and fiddliness, it is not.  But even with all this, it is an excellent game.  You need a few plays though to appreciate the game.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
Card Game con aggiunta della parte di Dice Draft.
Le meccaniche ricordano Magic, ogni giocatore sceglie uno dei dadi comuni che si lanciano a inizio turno, i dadi a seconda della faccia permettono di raccogliere energie dei 4 tipi, aumentare il livello di proprie carte in gioco, pescarne di nuove ecc..
Per giocare le carte della mano occorre pagare il relativo costo in energie indicato prendendole da quelle disponibili. Ogni carta ha poi un suo effetto istantaneo, permanente o one-shot.
Ogni X turni avviene un cambio stagione, che fa cambiare anche i dadi lanciati a inizio turno e di conseguenza anche le tipologie di energie prodotte ecc..
E' possibile variare la difficoltà di gioco aggiungendo o rimuovendo determinate carte.

This "downtime" kills it for her.

RATING: 6
These kinds of card games are not usually my jam, but I enjoyed this game. I'm not usually a bit fan of this style of game with all these cards and then all the combos and effects you have to keep in your brain every turn. And there is a bit of player interaction stuff, which usually isn't my thing either. So pretty good game if I was still able to enjoy myself!

I'm also not a massive fan of games for the first play that involve a drafting section that determines base cards for the whole game. And then following that, you need to separate out the cards you drafted into the 3 phases of the game.. so it's pretty easy having never played before to do a bad draft followed by a bad sorting of the cards into the three phases and shoot yourself in the foot before the game 'begins'. Luckily cards seem to work well with each other so you're not totally doomed...

RATING: 8
Card drafting with resource management and action selection. Draft a deck of 9 cards, gain 3 of those cards of your choice in each of 3 years. Dice determine actions available every round. Minimal player interaction with base cards, but advanced cards increase this. Plays great with 3 or 4, suffers from zero sum problems in 2 player. Plays in about an hour.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Inventive illustrations, great components (I want to shake those chunky beautiful dice at my 1Ed Quarriors cripples and scream ‚Why couldn’t you be a bit like THIS!’) and a fantasy theme makes this a possible dream game for me. Reading the rules just increased my interest, although I saw the possibility that the actual game might still disappoint due to unbalanced or uninteresting cards – obviously, these are the lynchpins in this design.

After about ten games, most of them two-player, I’m absolutely not disappointed. We’ve only dipped into the basic set of 30 cards so far, but even without the advanced cards, there is an interesting development in what cards we draft and how we use them during the game. Initially underutilized stuff is now a very hot drafting commodity, and vice versa. An example of the latter is Filgrim, the crystal stealing apprentice, who seemed to be pretty strong until we found various ways to delay crystal scoring well into the 2nd year to hinder his thievery.

If the advanced cards prove to be as well developed as the basic set, my rating of this will certainly increase, and if all players have some games under their belt, this is certainly not just a good game for two. Highly recommended.

RATING: 6
First Impression 7.5

I think it could safely be categorized close to CCGs such as MtG or Yugioh gameplay speaking: you need to collect some kind of resources to play the cards at hand, and once the card is played it have some kind of effect that give you points or affect other players points. What gives this game a nice twist to me is the deck building phase and the limited amount of cards (and the art is pretty nice too).

The game was well received in my gaming group, and more enthusiastically by the youngest (two boys around 12). Surprisingly to me my dad (65+) liked the game quite well.

To address some of the recurrent arguments against this game: 
+ I would say that the game can be played with 3 or even 4 players and be ejoyable, no doubt the last year will get pretty long turns, but anyway with the right group it maintains its fun. 
+ Yes, the game maybe is too long
+ Yes, the score (crystals) track is awful (why so tiny?)
+ I would say that the tracks in the player boards are not the best designed ones, as it is quite easy to knock down the cubes that keep the summoning gauge or the "cheats" count, and is not easy to remember the original position
+ You need to try the game with the building deck phase, as the basic decks won't give you the full picture, but I recommend to play the first game with the basic pre-builded decks

So far we haven't tryed the advanced cards, but I'm hoping to play again soon.

---------------------------------------------------------

Updated: 6.0

I can see where the fun is... but is not for me. Some of the things that don't work for me are:
+ Language barrier: being in a Spanish speaking country where English is not that much common, is hard to get people into playing.
+ Only a 2 player game: I retract about stating that the game can be enjoyable with 3 or even 4, it just drag a lot in the final years and will have huge down-times
+ Not fun interaction: yes, there's player interaction in the game, but for me is not fun interaction. This is not a problem in many games, but in games where the down-time can get quite long, at least I want something interesting to look at.
+ Super long card chains: I think that most people that do love this game is because of this very reason, but me, I don't enjoy it...

Traded away, I hope to a better home where it would get lots of plays!

RATING: 6
One play of the basic (non-introductory) game isn't enough for a proper rating on this one, but ratings are a journey and it's a 6 for now.

Seasons gives you enough rope to hang yourself right out of the gate, in the form of choosing your cards for each year. I made some good and some poor choices, but went last in the first year; my dice didn't let me assemble an engine to make the rest of the game go smoother.

Once I did get some things going, I was far behind with no synergies to look forward to. All around, a frustrating play -- but one where I could see an arc of development, of gaining fluency with the deck and the rhythms of the game, in future plays.

The "learning cliff," for me, left a bit of a sour taste. Particularly as it came on the heels of diving straight into Splender and having a blast right out of the gate, which is generally my preferred type of first-play experience.

RATING: 7
So far I have - as of 2013-11-16 - played twice using the intro rules: prebuilt deck, cards 1-30 only. It's taking quite a long time to play because of a lack of familiarity with the cards and strategies. I've enjoyed both games quite a bit and look forward to getting fast enough to add the draft and the new cards.
Added another two plays. You definitely have to figure out how best to develop your hand over the three years - when are you going to play cards and when are you going to Transmute. Is your hand a cards hand or a Transmute hand or a balanced one? Missing out on Summoning Gauge when you need it is really bad. Really. Bad. Next game I think we'll try drafting.
As of 17/01/2014 I have tried both drafting and the additional 20 cards from the base game. I like it, but I'm beginning to feel that there's significant luck elements that I'm not so happy about. For instance, if I draft a card and it's no good to me or even counter-productive, but my opponent draws a brilliant synergistic card, how can my skill counter that? Or, if I roll the Dice of Despair (or indeed, both of them) and never get an improved roll, what's the point? Or if I play crap all game and then draft both copies of the Sceptre of Greatness in year III and those 80 points win me the game, dragging me from last to first, isn't that just luck? Not sure whether it needs way more plays to ensure that the best player wins, or whether it is too subject to luck.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Draft, play and manipulate cards and dice to be the greatest wizard.

Plays: 8.
Type: VP race = a game where you race to collect victory points (fastest/most). There’s a little bit of progressive efficiency, but not enough to call it a VP engine.
Complexity: Medium. This shouldn’t be the first deck building/card drafting game you play.
# of players: Probably best with 2. Downtime will increase with each player.
Playing time: 30 mins per player for the base game.
Player interaction: Opportunity prevention – drafting of cards and dice. Competition for unlimited resources – power and crystals. Some cards allow light conflict.
Randomness: Medium. The initial card draft and the dice draft during the game are low randomness, but you get more cards from the top of the draw pile during the game that break the symmetry.

Replayability: Average. You won’t get to see all the cards in the deck during one game and every game will feature different card combinations. This means every game will in one sense be unique. However, every game will be pretty samey in how you go about acquiring points. There aren’t wildly diverse strategies. 
Fun: Low. The fun lies in the decision making of hand and board management.
Memorability: Low, it’s a VP race that doesn’t create stories.

Thematic integration: Decent. The art does a good job communicating the theme. It’s still pretty obvious you’re just collecting points though, you never feel like a wizard.
Components: Good and bad. The art is very good and has a unique storybook fantasy feel to it. This is probably the biggest draw of the game and might even have players going “oooh” and “aaah”. For some reason this is complemented with not very functional player boards and basic wooden cubes. With such great art, you feel let down that the same attention clearly did not go into the other components. Cubes on laminated cardboard with tiny numbers just invites ambiguity from the slightest nudge.

Who will like this: Gamers that already own other card drafting or deck building games and want an alternative to play now and then.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A

RATING: 8.5
A draftolás a 7 Csodát, a kockavezérelt akciók Feldet, a kártyák kombózása pedig a Dominiont juttatta eszembe közben, a mechanizmusok tehát kiváló "donoroktól" lettek koppintva, de ez egyáltalán nem érződik, tökéletesen belesimul a játékmenetbe minden.

Dizájnra is gyönyörű egyébként, aki látta közelről a Lords of Xiditet, annak ismerős lesz (de a kinézeten kívül - hiába készítették ugyanazok - semmi köze nincs hozzá).

Még mielőtt rásülne a bélyeg, hogy több kiváló játék jól működő mechanizmusát gyúrták csak egybe, muszáj megemlítenem az egyedi fordulóváltást: a játékosok által nem választott - megmaradt - kockán szereplő pontok számától függően telik az idő, azaz haladunk előre az évszakok kerekén 1-2, vagy egyszerre akár 3 hónapot is. Remek.

Nem bonyolult, elsőre átlátható, amolyan "light" gamer játék. Annak ellenére, hogy a 7 csodánál bonyolultabb, simán mehetne egyébként a családi kategóriába.

A pontgyűjtés egyébként egészen egyszerű: szerezzünk energiatokeneket a kocka segítségével, amiket beváltva vagy közvetlenül pontot kaphatunk vagy kártyá(ka)t tehetünk le belőlük, amikkel kombózva szintén pontokat kaphatunk. Kb. ennyi.

A játék kiváló, mindenképpen a "muszáj kipróbálni" kategória. Magyar kiadását már évek óta nem kapni, erősen OOP, de akinek nem jelent gondot az angol, megugorhatja a nemzetközi kiadását is (kártyák miatt erősen nyelvfüggő).

És hogy negatívumot is mondjak róla: interakció minimális (néhány kártya van csupán hatással a többi játékosra), illetve jelen van a szerencse is, de ez utóbbi nem jobban, mint a 7 Csodában, vagy mondjuk egy Castles of Burgundy-ban (előbbiben a draft, utóbbiban a kártyavezérelt akciók miatt).
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.75
An odd game. The dice are satisfyingly hefty and are appealing. I'm not wild about the artwork, but it's unique and certainly isn't BAD, it's just not quite my style. An awful lot of the game hinges on the initial draft since card drawing opportunities are few and far between. Finding and exploiting synergies in your cards is key here. Detractors says that the draft is the only major decision-making point in the game. I'm not sure I can disagree with that or, if that's true, I'm not whether that makes it a bad game or not. A few plays in I'm finding it pretty fun, more than the sum of its parts.

RATING: 9
Im Wald der Jahreszeiten treffen sich die großen Magier von Xidit zusammen, um in einem magischen Wettstreit den neuen Erzmagier zu ermitteln. Jeder Spieler verkörpert einen dieser Magier. Seasons (dt. Jahreszeiten) wird über drei (Spiel-)Jahre gespielt und die wechselnden Jahreszeiten sind eine Kernmechanik des Spiels. Die unterschiedlichen jahreszeiten bringen Vor- und Nachteile. Zu Beginn des Spiels draften die Spieler 9 Karten und verteilen diese anschließend auf die drei (Spiel-)Jahre. Nach und nach beschwören die Spieler diese Machtkarten und rufen so mächtige Artefakte oder Verbündete an ihre Seite. Änhlich wie 7 Wonders ist Seasons ein nahezuperfektes Card-Drafting Spiel und kommt bei mir immer mal wieder auf den Tisch. Die vielen Machtkarten erlauben unterschiedlichste Strategien, vor allem mit den beiden Erweiterungen. Seasons spielt sich in jeder Partie erfrischen anders und das hübsch gestaltete Spielmaterial ist klasse!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
Fun little game and I enjoyed the interaction between dice, tokens, cards and points.   I felt the cards' cost vs. power was balanced for the most part and never felt like I was locked into the doldrums of a random-draw pattern matching exercise like some card-drafting games play.  My biggest complaint is with the cards that bump up one person's points while deducting other player's points for inevitable actions, such as at the change of a season, a change of a round, or the play of a card.  A person playing this card needs not invest any more resources into the card while other player cannot do anything to prevent the effect of the card.   The season will pass, the round will end, inevitably you must play a card to compete.  A balance may be to only add points to the one player without deducting points from the others, but I think the game would be tighter overall with this flavor of card entirely removed.

Way better than Dominion and I would try it again

RATING: 7
As wizards, you draft 9 cards like Fairy Tale/7 Wonders. Then you have to group your cards into three for when they will come out in the 3 years of the game.

Every turn, dice are rolled. Players select a die which governs their action: get energy, get VP, increase summoning gauge (number of cards a player can have in play) or draw more cards. Players can also use bonus actions at the cost of losing VPs. The die not chosen governs the speed of the game as you cycle around the year.

This game has good mechanics. I have only played once, and enjoyed my first play. There is a bit of luck in that some cards are more powerful than others. I heard that the sweet spot for this game is 2-3 players, so I'm looking forward to play more games with this number. This is only an initial rating, and may go up with 2 player games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Magical themed card game for 2-4 players. Draft 9 cards and play them across three years, with each season yielding differing energy tokens (water, earth, wind and fire). Cards require varying costs of these energy resources to be put into play. Can be a lot of fun. 

PROS:
-Interesting card interactions.
-Multiple paths to victory.
-Each game varies dramatically.
-Requires skill on the go to adapt and respond to what other players do to stop you.
-Great artwork.
-Theme works well, especially the "seasons". 
-Good amount of player interaction despite the card game nature (familiar cards). 
-Players have  a lot of control over the game, being able to influence how fast the seasons progress.
-Time always moves forward at least one spot, so the game has a definite end point (can be prolonged but not indefinitely!).    
-How many cards you can play is limited by a summoning gauge that starts at zero, with the max being 15 cards you can play across the entire game. Very unique. 

CONS:
-Can be too long with four players and becomes a pain to keep track of all card interactions.
-Crystal board is a terrible way to keep track of currency. Its long and tedious and prone to error. Physical crystal tokens should have been provided. 
-Summoning gauge track is very small, should be bigger, possibly with a rotation device or sliding marker. Way too easy to make a mistake. 
-Some cards a little too powerful.
-Some cards very painful, make the game longer and remove some fun. 

Would not readily recommend to another player. To enjoy the game, I made the following changes:
-Add physical crystal currency. You will need x50 1-value crystals, x40 5-value crystals and x10 10-value crystals. Look for acrylic 'vase filler' and 'table scatter'. These do perfectly, are cheap and common wedding/ home decor supplies. 
-Buy both expansions and remove every 2nd copy of all cards, so there is 100% unique cards (i.e. no doubles). With the expansions, the deck is large enough after being cut in half. 
-Remove the following cards entirely: Twist of Fate, Igramul the Banisher, Wonderous Chest, Mesodae's Lantern. Fist two cards became useless because of the modified deck of completely unique cards. Last two cards removed as we found any ability to gain x3 crystals each round too powerful, being a dominant unbeatable strategy. Have also considered removing Arcano Leech and Figrim the Avaricious. These require annoying account keeping and make the game less fun if summoned very early.  

NOTE: Current rating based on the modifications above. Base game without modifications would be a ~8/10 for me. Depends on how much time you want to invest improving the game.

RATING: 5
Awfully underwhelming game.

1. The seasons are not individually important. Rarity of energies found through each are NOT enough to make them of any real consequence. Give us abilities to make one season more desirable than the next...or undesirable.

2. Limited interaction with opponent. It's solitaire, basically. What I do has little bearing on what my opponent does and vice versa.

3. "Transmutation." Why would they not allow players to transmute one energy for another? Why for crystals? They are sparingly used to pay for things and the cards give more victory points in the end...

4. Way too fast game sessions. In two turns, you can pass through three seasons. Again, they are meaningless. Cards are easy to get into play and I've NEVER run into having a hand at the end of the game. Neither has my opponent.

5. It's just not interesting. The card art is fine, but still has a feeling of worthlessness. Some cards are very powerful, while some have no value. Yet, there are duplicates of each one! WHY? My side of the table shouldn't mirror my opponent's. One of each card. THAT'S IT!

6. The upcoming expansion does exactly what I thought it would: add more dumb cards and a "purpose" for the little hole in the character card. Big deal. The game needs its core rules changed, not more awful cards.

7. Speaking of cards, FIFTEEN slots? Come on. May as well be unlimited if you're going to clutter the table with that many.

RATING: 7
Played it a bunch of times online before purchasing it and playing it live.

There's a potential lot to keep track of when playing in real life, especially if there's multiple cards with triggered actions, but it is fun nonetheless.

Update 1:  There's a couple of things that bother me about that have prevented me from bringing it out more often.

- There's a lot to keep track of when your plauteau of cards gets bigger and bigger.

- Art is beautiful but graphic design could've been better.  There's several cards with a ton of text that feels like they are paste-ups as the text runs longer than the centered space for it.  

- Drafting at beginning of game clearly sets the difference between experts and newbies.  There's no way for newbies to draft well in their first games, thus setting themselves up for an unfavorable position for the rest of the game.

-  Game is really meant for 2 players as it runs long and there's a lot of stuff going on to keep track of.

I think I have better games for any amount of players this offers and I'm only really keeping it because the artwork and dice are fantastic.  

Well, I can always go play it at boardgamearena and be finished much faster than in a real tabletop game.

RATING: 8
Each time I've had this game on the table people walking by ask about it.  Unfortunately, there is no succinct way to describe it.  There are so many different mechanics thrown in that it likens to several games while being distinct from them all.  It has a drafting mechanic for on the fly deck building.  It has a dice rolling and selection mechanic.  It has a resource management mechanic.  It has card chaining.  This is definitely a game for gamers.  So far I have only played with the base set of cards, and I'm excited to see what the advanced cards will do to keep the game interesting.

RATING: 9
2-4 wizards play three seasons (each season having a set of cards) playing spells and collecting crystals.  Each season is split into 4 seasons with each season having a different set of die to determine the energy people can choose for the round.  Summoning stars determine how many cards you may have in front of you in play.  

The game actually does remind me a bit of Magic the Gathering.  I love that they give you some 'starter decks' instead of using the drafting method to make your three decks.  Towards the end of the game there is quite a bit going on and coming up with a great way to maximize your points by using cards and selling energy is just brain burn-ey enough to remain fun, challenging, and rewarding.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7.5
The production is fantastic, the graphic design is outstading, even for the usual French high standards. Everything inside and outside looks great. 

It's a game about drafting cards, then getting resources to play these cards and comboing them. The combos are powerful and they seem able to create big differences. The gameflow is fast, but there's downtime and the last turns are quite longer.

There's a bit of luck involved, but the draft and selection of cards minimize it and make the learning curve steep. Once the cards are drafted, the strategic part doesn't seem to be deep, but there will be many tactic considerations when choosing the dice and ocasionally drawing more cards and adapting your strategy.

Overall, I found it to be very very fun. I think it excels at 2 players and handles 3 players, but 4-player games should be avoided.

RATING: 9
As of November 2013, this is one of the best games I've ever played.  It has beautiful components and artwork, great mechanics, and is actually incredibly simple.  This game looks intimidating to some, but it is incredibly simple to play and involves a lot of forward-thinking-strategy.

My girl, who isn't much of a gamer, loves this game.  She loves it win or lose.  So if you have those people who you want to sit down and enjoy a game with, this seems to be that game (I actually bought this because people said it played great with 2 players and that their girl's also loved it).

The game has card drafting at the start of the game and players prepare starter hands for the 3 year tournament.  Each turn a player rolls the dice and each player takes turns choosing a die.  And then each player takes turn playing cards from their hand that they can afford.

The mechanics work smoothly and most times I play, the others want to keep playing!

RATING: 7
It's a Euro game, no question, which is probably why I enjoy it as much as I do. As such, mechanically speaking, it's brilliant. It also looks thematic, but once inside the player will discover that quite a few of the beautifully illustrated cards have quirky names and lovely pictures that have nothing to do with what they actually accomplish when played. For example, why does "summoning" Lewis Greyface (a white rabbit who holds a wooden club menacingly behind his back as if he's about to secretly attack) allow me to copy but not steal my opponent's energy tokens? Why (thematically, not mechanically) does the Die of Malice give me two crystals every time I use it? etc. etc. In other words, for a game that looks so vividly thematic, it may be a bit of a letdown for some to discover that it's all actually a facade, an extremely thin coat of paint for a mechanically solid game. It was for me at least.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Some interesting stuff going on here. I like how the dice draft works each turn - roll and each player takes one in turn, the one left over determines how fast the game clock advacnces. The set of dice used change each turn, or each other turn depending on how fast the clock goes, and each set offers different resources, so as the seasons change the likelihood of collecting each resource type alters. You spend the resources to get your cards out, which provide either one-off or ongoing benefits. And before the game starts, you choose which 3 cards you'll be able to build in year 1, which 3 in year 2 and which 3 in year 3, providing some game-start planning. Haven't done the drafting yet a la 7 Wonders style, but that will be some pretty hefty pre-game gaming right there. Once you got going, turns were at a decent speed, as your cards don't change that much. Choose your die, get your resource, spend it if you can to get a card out. There's a die option to get more cards, so you're not limited to what you start with. There's obviously a fair bit of replay here given all the sorts of decks you can build, seeing how they work out, balancing resources vs effects. Easily worthy of more exploration.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5.5
I'm mixed on this game.  It's been on my radar for quite awhile and I was very interested in it.  I love its theme, card comboing, dice rolling, and actions to perform.  It's fairly easy to learn its rules.

This game isn't forgiving for newbies.  The starting cards to divide up to the three years can cost player if they don't know how easy or difficult they can come out and their power.

My main issue is that there is a wide difference in the cards' powers, with some being very overpowered.  One person got to build a free thirty point card while I had the same card and had to build it for 20 crystals, which rubbed me the wrong way.  The dice provide additional card draws, however overall card draws seemed very light.

Our four player game took a lot longer than what it felt like it should have taken.  You'd think its limited time track should have expired quicker.

I definitely want to play this again to see how the card strengths mitigate out, however overall I was disappointed.

RATING: 7
I'm not sure if this is a Drafting game or an Engine Builder.  You only work towards one goal: Get the most crystals.  It doesn't matter how you do this, just as long as you get the most.

Drafting is interesting in that you have to set up an early and late game strategy for yourself in the first ten minutes, and then the rest of the hour is spent drafting dice to assist you in getting cards out onto the board.

I still don't know if I even like this game or not, but I'm obsessed with trying to figure out what strategies are most viable and which cards are worth drafting over others.

RATING: 7
I liked this far less than I hoped I would.  The art and components are great...beautiful, even.  The game itself is fun to play, as well.  Where it suffers is in book-keeping.  Crystals, summoning levels, card effects (collect a crystal from each player, add to yours every time you enter a new season; add three crystals if you have four energy at the end of each round, etc. etc.)...I feel like it needs an app to keep track of one or more of these things.  But then, if you need an app to play the game, why not just play an app version of the game, right?  
It isn't that I don't like Seasons, I just wish it was less fiddly, and could be played a bit more casually.

RATING: 7.5
A strategic engine-building game with enough variation to keep it from getting stale. I like the drafting for cards to start the game, as you can really build up some powerful combos for yourself later in the game and get an idea of what other people might be doing based on what cards get drafted before you see that hand again. The dice-rolled options make sure you can't pursue a single strategy that you plan from the beginning, since you have to roll with what you're given to some degree.

There's not a whole lot of interaction, but there is some.

RATING: 8.5
Medium-weight combo-building card game that mixes up an amazing artwork, stunning production quality and dice/energy/transmutation mechanics that blend in with the game nicely. The draft system makes you play with what you have on hand/table, as opposed to building your strategy on "what will/may come". This shifts the weight towards metagame during the draft, but it doesn't take long and introduces few interesting options - you can deduce some of your opponent's cards and you can deny some.

Hands down, one of the best games of 2012. Several ways of achieving the victory. Keep in mind that it's not M:tG or Netrunner and it's meant for slightly different crowd. You can get anyone to learn Seasons quickly and play it with you. Hardcore gamers that seek mind-melting deckbuild challenge will min/max it too quickly and whine about randomness. Others will have great fun with it.

The only complaint I have is a bit too little player interaction. There are cards that can provide that (some pretty ingenious!), but it usually doesn't feel like it really changes things score-wise. I'd love to see more take mana/crystals cards and direct opponent's table interaction (steal cards, decrease counters, and whatnot). 

And, oh, the box insert is a masterpiece and conquered my heart. Every game producer should follow this.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 4
Pros:
* Lovely art and great production quality.
* Pretty quick setup.
* Excellent box insert. Even fits sleeved cards!

Cons:
* Need to learn the cards well in order to really play "Phase 1" (drafting).
* Too many moving parts and things to remember (fiddly).
* While scores are mostly open, there's just too damn much to count up to really know how you're doing.
* No interesting player interaction (at least not in the first 30 cards). The draft might count as interaction, but only if you know the cards well enough to do hate drafting [UPDATE: Based on Rahdo's comments, it sounds like there are a lot of "attacky" cards available so perhaps there's more interaction in the advanced cards]

= 2013-07-07 - R:4, Adding to Trade list.

First play. 2 player. Used "Apprentice" (no draft) starter rules.

Underwhelmed. Based on the games we've rated highly, I think it's fair to say we prefer more abstract games with a lot less text on the cards and a lot fewer combos. We actually *like* combos in games like Dominion (which we disliked for other reasons), Blue Moon City and Babel or chain reactions like you get in Gheos and Kahuna, but those games do it with a much smaller number of different cards. I suspect if you could refer to a game as "MtG: The Board Game" we're probably not going to like it.

By midgame, it was getting annoying keeping up with all the persistent effects cards and the collecting of necessary energy just felt like drudgery.

Towards the end of the game we were purposefully leaving the die with the most pips on it behind just to make the game end sooner.

At almost no point in the game did I care what the other person was doing. Sometimes she would sacrifice an energy to cost me 4 points, but I couldn't make myself care. She may as well have been moving values around in my spreadsheet.

Boring. Fiddly. Tedious.

I'm giving this one a 4, but I'm also admitting this it likely an excellent game for people who like MtG style combos, drafting or victory point engines.

RATING: 7
Enjoyable, relaxed game for two. It's beautiful, it has quite a few meaningful choices (although mostly during the draft phase), it can be tense depending on the cards drafted. If you love dice and cards, "Seasons" provides both in spades. Thanks to the way the cards work, there are many interesting combinations and after almost twenty plays I still discover new ways to utilize my hand.

However, if you want to enjoy this game, you need to accept the randomness - randomness that can sometimes cost you the game (or win you one). The draft phase works well to partially balance things, but the cards you draw during play are just pure luck. Sometimes you will get something that will dramatically change the outcome and there is no way to protect yourself if you're on the wrong end of the stick. Be warned!

Other than that, really fun, loveable game.

RATING: 7
Played quite a lot during the summer, it is quite portable, and also playable on a beach. 
The draft before the actual game defines 90% of the card pool you have to work with durinng the game, and sorting these into three groups of three (two groups become available only later in the game) presents also interesting decisions, which will have a direct effect, how you build up your engine during the game. 
The players are always throwing one more dice than the number of players and then choose one from this pool in turn order. The pips on the remaining dice dictates the speed of the game, which is quite interesting, since the changing seasons define the scarcity and the availability of the resources. 
Overall the game hase exceptionally beautyful components, chunky big dices, and the illustrations on the cards - albeit anime-style - are beautyful. But still the game feels a bit shallow. There is a small amount of take that, but it is on an acceptable level. 
I would play it under the right circumstances, and due to its portability and weight it remains in my collcection. 
Worth to mention, that it is a good 2-player game, with more the AP could be a significant drawback.

RATING: 8
I have a love/hate relationship with Seasons. I love it because individually, each of the mechanics and cards are fantastic. Drafting dice and cards, the lovely artwork, etc. All great! That being said, I'm just not convinced the game is entirely balanced. In the future I may take out certain cards from the deck to ensure a more even distribution of abilities. The orange cards especially seem to wreak a nasty kind of havoc on players, not to mention slow down the game with constant micro-managing of crystals.

Overall: I really want to love this game. There's so many good things to note, but for my game group's personal play style it may require tweaking.

RATING: 9.7
Great feeling when playing this game.  It's on my top 10 list to stay.  A complete pleasure to play.  The three major mechanisms (card draft at beginning, dice rolling/selection, and putting cards in play...developing combos) are all well done and all work well together.

Sure, it's a little fiddly in the final year when you get out lots of cards that interact with each other, but it's not overly difficult to keep everything organized.

At least a few times in each game you'll hear:  "WAIT!! I was supposed to......" and then you have to decide if it's too late, or the adjustment will be allowed.
The terminology:  collect 'energy' to turn it into 'crystals' is not at all confusing (as some reviews would lead you to believe).

I also got some 'crystals' at Goodwill that we use instead of the fiddly scoreboard.  It has enhanced the game for us.  Cards that involve giving crystals to other players are somehow more fun as you physically slide the crystals across the table....rather than 'adjust' the scoreboard.  Here's a link to the thread where photos are posted of the physical crystals: http://www.boardgamegeek.com/article/16815039#16815039

Great game.  Recommended.  I have not tried it 4P, but it works great 2P or 3P.  I think 4P would be too long....with too much downtime.

A good implementation at BoardGameArena gives a great feeling for the game...and it's kinda nice to have it do the accounting, so you don't forget stuff (at change of season, etc...)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 2
I can understand why people would enjoy this game.  It's got some interesting card combos and could be very competitive once you fully understand the system and all the cards.  I'm not willing to invest my time and effort into playing a game like this - or any game where you have to invest in a system (think MTG, Android: Netrunner, X-Wing: Miniatures, etc).

As a new player this game is going to be rough for you.  If you are playing against an experienced player you are probably not going to enjoy it.  It's definitely one you'd need to stick with for the long run to get any satisfaction out of it.  You're going to need to understand what every card does, what every combo is, which tactics work, etc.

There are so many other games I'd rather play than this.  My rating is quite low because I don't ever want to play this game again and I did not enjoy my experience with this game.  I can, however, understand how others enjoy this game.

RATING: 6
Played via Boardgamearena.com

My impressions on my initial game of Seasons differed from the initial drafting at the beginning of the game to actual play i.e. choosing a die and taking any appropriate actions. I greatly enjoyed the drafting and trying to achieve synergies between the different card choices. The actual play felt rather flat because there is minimal interaction, and minimal interesting decisions. 

The initial detraction is that the theme is pasted on; this is no different than many other Euro games. I felt a disconnect from the initial drafting (and lovely artwork) to the actual game play in choosing a die and taking appropriate actions. Any interesting decisions were taken at the start of the game, and game-play was actually waiting to be able to take an action. 

My main bone of contention is that unlike a deck construction or drafting game, where you are receiving cards as you play, in Seasons all the decisions are largely made upfront, and you are using actions from the die to implement your strategy based upon the initial draft. However, unlike a deck construction game in which you are interacting with your opponent, there is no real interaction in Seasons.

Crystals are also given out like candy, leading to a points salad type experience.

Playing face to face may be better because the components (from reviews) look gorgeous, and the die are large, chunky and colourful providing a tactile experience. Also, the game plays quite fast, and I would estimate 45 mins to an hour face to face.

RATING: 7.2
Love love love the system.  Hate Hate Hate most of the content.  This game is extremely slippery slope, which is fine in a game as long as it a) ends quickly  or b) allows the losing player to shoot the moon with an alternate win condition.  Unfortunately neither of these things exist in Seasons.  You are forced to play the game for an additional 10-15 minutes after you've lost.   The worst part is the number of spells that allow you to cripple your opponent in some fashion.  By the end of my first game, any action I took would result in a net loss for me.   I had all of these abilities, but could not use ANY of them.   While I agree that this just means I drafted poorly, the fact still remains that the game is unfun if I am not even able to play any of my abilities.

I went in with hesitance,
after a few rounds I loved it,
by the end of the game I hated it.

"Ok, so you are getting 1 energy for your turn?  Ok so that means I get 3 VPs because of upgrade X, you lose a 2 VPs because of upgrade Y, and I get 5 more VPs at the end of every one of your turns because of all this other crap I got."

I also agree with the overall thoughts on the length.  The game's story arc is similar to Magic the Gathering, but it's 3-4 X the length.  I think 1/3 of the length could be shaved off easily, and it would be a lot more enjoyable.

Another option would be to simply have the game end after a certain point differential.  IE: if a player has 50 points more than his opponent on the board at any point, he wins instantly.

RATING: 6.5
in the deep past, my bane was win-hungry jerks teaching half the game and then winning due to a rule they forgot to mention. years later, i avoided games full of tiny text on cards that might or might not suddenly and drastically change the game state. more recently, i just don't see the point in bothering to get briefly familiar with a card-text-driven game when we'll probably only play it once. as such, i'd avoided Seasons. after having played... well, i see why it's a classic. and, i suppose the card draft helps mitigate some of the wondering what's in my opponent's hand (although there's still a ton of reading cards that may crush or or might only crumple). it was fun, but not really a game i want to own and bring out with non-gamers, although i have seen it magically work before (maybe they played Magic?). it's heavier than 7 Wonders, but less instantly accessible. i might rather play this than Ethnos, but not Race for the Galaxy.

RATING: 9

RATING: 9
Great game with great artwork and great mechanics. It has a lot of interlocking and well though-out systems that tie together seamlessly. Drafting, dice based action selection, tableau/engine building and, of course, passage of time that influences it all. Base game is pretty self-contained, I can see it being played for decades. Drafting and card combos galore make it feel like a full CCG in a single box, with powerful combos and occasional lucky draws, yet mechanics are very euro-ish and solid.
Expansions add a lot of cool cards and new combos, but other stuff there (one-time use tokens and optional rules cards) looks more like fan-made content and a bunch of random homerules than like properly designed developement of the game. It's ok for variety, but ultimately I don't care about it and I believe it's there just to fill the box. But if you really like the game, the cards are worth it.
Also, I have played this game online on BGA first, and decided to buy it afterwards. Game publishers that don't allow their content online are just shooting themselves in the leg.

RATING: 5
Pretty good, but not sure what all the hype was about.  It feels very much like Macao-lite, in that you are under pressure to play all your cards, trying to plan ahead to get the resources to play them, and deciding which order to play them to make the most out of the combo effects.  I don't care so much for the drafting at the beginning - I like more in Macao how you can see some of what cards will be available in the future and use the turn order mechanic to decide how important it is to have first pick on cards - this let's you jump into the game faster. There is also a lot more planning ahead in Macao - trying to the have the right resources at the right time - whereas in Seasons, you're more at the whim of what rolls, with a little planning based on what the odds are of getting certain resources each season. I wish the seasons theme was stronger (hence the name of the game) - you never really feel like you're in summer or winter.

Overall a light game for non-gamers, who, after repeated plays, will perhaps manage to see its hidden depth.

RATING: 6
Differentiates itself from the other card-drafting games by spicing up the rest of the game so that it can pass itself off as a rare 2-player card-drafter. I'm not really a fan of card-drafting (high overhead for new players, rewards memory and spot-the-difference), but I warmed up to the rest of the game.

The vibrant artwork showed off the game's soul. The multi-choice dice drafting each turn was interactive while providing interesting decisions (what's not taken advances the season at a different rate). The setting is thoughtful, with resources corresponding to different seasons, and the paint job makes sense. Picking a strategy by splitting your cards into 3 piles to play with didn't seem as agonizing as I had expected or hoped, but is still interesting nonetheless.

This is a much healthier game than the shitpile that is The Castles of Burgundy. This one has interactive dice-drafting, and you actually build something meaningful over the course of 3 rounds. Seems like the logical next step for King of Tokyo fans.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1
It's game that leaves a player with an existential question at the end of the session: [i]"what the hell were we doing for the last 60 minutes?"[/i]

Seasons brings all sorts of things to the table and unto the table, as it is a tableau builder - there are dice, cards, interlocking power and many things with no rhyme nor reason. Firstly synergy of anything with anything is a bit clunky - do this, to do that, so that over there can combo with the thing over over here. Basically the game gives you lots of stuff to juggle plus immediate,  intermediate and long range plans (by splitting your hand in 3 parts of the game). 

The problem is, when the game is over and you look over what have you been juggling all the stuff for, the game says nothing, shows nothing, is nothing. There's no narrative, no theme, there's no even enjoyment of having build something as - a) not much was done, b) what was done doesn't look like anything cohesive. What this game is about is anybody's guess. And thus the worst insult it throws at the players are these cutesy, nowadays more and more common french comicbook style illustration, that try to give an impression the game is about something, anything. It's not. It's themeless. Might as well be Mondrian art on all the cards (which would be bold and kinda honest), but it seems Ze french designerz love ze cute little creturez and clunky card synergies. It reminded me of Gosu with more stuff and more pictures and less cohesion which would bind the bits together. And the forced cutesyness. 

So: it's a juggling + optimisation game, and as there are dice and a variety of cards,  there's a lot to do without it getting calculable. Problem is - there's no theme, no narrative, you're generating a lot of points, but hadn't built anything, neither an engine nor a proper tableau. Yet, the game keeps you busy for the sake of keeping you busy (not to notice the chasm the size of English Channel where the theme ought to be) - there's a lot of bookkeping with so many effects being triggered by this or that. It's kinda like Terra Mystica in that regard - keeps you busy so you don't notice you're not doing anything. It's also shorter than TM and nicer looking. But as pointless. It's a cute game that keeps you involved, just when it's over nothing remains - no feelings, no story, nothing.

rating down to 1.0: because it's a completely themeless collection of mechanisms with no rhyme nor reason and yet some think it has a theme. What theme? What is it about? Yeah. I don't want such abuse of theming seen in boardgames that's why 1.0 [which for me means: hope nobody makes such games again]

RATING: 9
Seasons is a simple drafting game with a strong cool factor due to its lush illustrations and pretty components. While the initial draft may seem like where most of the game takes place, there are plenty of decisions to be made throughout the game regarding the order in which cards are played and which dice to draft.

I've upped my ratings for Seasons over time as there is quite a lot about the game to discover, such as when is the best time to play particular cards and dealing with the metagame. There are issues of imbalanced cards, but I can live with those - I play with the fixed versions of the underpowered Balance of Ishtar and Idol of the Familiar, although Temporal Boots is still a bit overpowered.

Arranging the cards which have been summoned according to their effects is important, as there is a high chance that players will miss out on important effects as the game wears on. Seasons works well with 2 and 3 players, but I would avoid it with 4 as the downtime can be frustrating.

RATING: 6
[2013-01-19]
Amint megláttam, hogy kockás játék rögtön érdekelni kezdett, mindenkitől csak jót hallottam róla és tulajdonképpen egy tényleg jó játékról beszélhetünk, de nem igazán az én stílusom sajnos. Kicsit King of Tokyo, hatalmas kockák, gyönyörű kártyák, kicsit Dominion, kicsit Gosu, lapok, amiket kombózni kell. És ez utóbbi az, ami nem igazán az én asztalom. Több körön át a kezdőjátékos dob az adott évszak kockáival és egymás után választunk ki egyet magunknak. 4 jelölőt gyűjtünk, amiből a játék elején kapott, választott 9 kártyát játszhatjuk le magunknak. A kártyaszám nőhet a játék folyamán, célunk velük kristályok, a győzelmi pontok gyűjtése. Minden kártyából kettő van, amik sajnos nyelvfüggőek, de 2-3 játék után megtanulhatók. 2-3 fő út vezet a győzelemhez. Az alapkártyákkal talán nem lenne benne túl sok játék, de kiegészítőkkel már jóval több. A játék nekem nem tetsző részei: 4-en a játék már rendesen elhúzódhat, kicsit szerintem hosszabb is a kelleténél, de kell ez a hosszúság a lapok kombóinak megvalósítása miatt. 4-en már sok a holtidő, ezért egyértelműen 2-en és 3-an jó játszani. A szerencsének rendesen szerepe van a kockák miatt és mivel összesen 2 újradobókártya van, ezért jelentős, sőt az sem biztos, hogy ugyanannyiszor kezd minden játékos. Talán több újradobó lehetőséggel lehetne ezen kompenzálni. Mivel rengeteg pont gyűjthető, ezért ezt kis kockákkal és győzelmi pont jelelő táblával oldották meg, ami könnyen elmozdulhat. Túl sok pont van a játékban, túl sokszor kell a pontjelzőkkel bíbelődni. Összességében ez inkább komoly játék a kockák ellenére is, mint partyjáték. Nem rossz, akinek a Gosu, King of Tokyo, Dominion bejön az mindenképp próbálja ki.

Regardless, I'd still like a copy, because it's a fun and clever game.

RATING: 8
2 k
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
[u]First play, 3er [/u]

[b]--- Game Summary ---[/b]
The game is played over 3 rounds (years). A board tracks time in each year, using a 12-stop counter divided into 4 seasons (the specific time is indicated with a token). That board also shows the types of resources available that season, and the conversion rates for each resource (into crystals = VP). Place the (n+1) dice (different for each season) next to the appropriate part of the board. Player boards have storage space for 7 resources, indicators for 4 extra abilities (each use, to a max of x4, costs you more VP at end of game), and a track to show display capacity (i.e., the max number of cards you may have in play). 

Start the game by drafting 9 cards (we played basic game, with specific hands). Each player then divides them into 3 groups of 3, to use for each of the 3 game years. Cards cost various combinations of resources, and give you various bonuses (e.g., VP at end of game, in-game powers like converting your resources into VP, taking other players VP away, move the time counter, etc.).

Each round, the starting player rolls the n+1 dice for the current season (all of which have unique distributions of faces). Then each player selects a die. Then, take your turn. The die faces may provide you with 1-2 resources, or a new card from the deck; advance your card capacity; and/or allow you to convert resources into VP. You can also use powers on any cards in your display. The die faces also have 1-3 dots; advance the time counter that many steps on the time tracker. If you advance to a new year, add the appropriate cards to your hand. If you advance into year 4, the game ends -- most VP (on track + from cards in display - penalty of 5VP for each card left in hand) wins!

[b]--- Comments ---[/b]
That was a lot of fun! And I found out that it's available for online play at [url=http://www.boardgamearena.com]BGA[/url] so I will definitely be playing it again! They had only 60 copies or so flown in for the show, and those sold out on the first day.

The game has hand management (both in splitting the cards at the start, and in playing them during the game), resources management, action-selection (die selection), ways to fiddle with the pace of the game (by moving the time counter around, as well as when picking which die to leave unchosen), card-combinations for the CCG-lovers ... lots of little things woven together in a way that makes the game flow well. Like those CCGs, much of the intricacy (and rules headaches?) is going to come from the combination of cards you get and play. The randomness of the dice is there, but I don't think it's too much; there are usually useful choices, and everyone gets first pick in rotation. 

Looking forward to playing this more!

---------
[u]Update after several more plays online[/u]

You know what? This has convinced me -- I just don't like drafting! It's a neat idea, an equalizer of sorts when you have cards of varying powers and different combinations. But I'm just no good at it! I didn't like it way back when, when I played Magic and I don't like it now! It's a bit better in 7 Wonders, where you're simply playing the card right away, but I don't like having to figure out on-the-fly which combinations are going to work, and how I should construct my deck! I'd much rather take some time up front to construct my deck, then play it out. Sort of like in Magic: I much preferred the simple Sealed Deck format -- do the best with what you're given -- rather than the popular drafting versions. Maybe that partially explains my ambivalence towards Dominion and similar games ....

So, good game but I'll drop my rating a bit from the initial 7.5 -- it's just not going to hold interest for me in the long run.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Right now, this is my favorite of the lighter fare of games I own.  The gameplay is simple and easy to teach.  You draft a set of nine cards that you'll get in groups of three throughout the game and each turn you draft the result of a die roll and take actions based on your collection of cards and dice.  The winner is the person with the most victory points at the end of the game.  See, easy.

What I love about it is there is a lot of depth and a decent amount of variety just under the surface there.  Players have a small amount of control over the progression of the game which can fit well into certain strategies.  Each season of the game comes with different availability of resources used in the game so a good amount of planning is involved.  And there are 100 cards (2 each of 50 different ones) that do a large variety of different things.  Some directly attack your opponent, some provide resources, and some make up some pretty interesting combos (my favorite, which my wife used against me, is playing the card that allows you to draw 4 and play one for free...and getting the 30 VP card that costs 20 to put into play).

I would also like to point out that the production of this game is FANTASTIC!  The components are all very high quality and the artwork is gorgeous with a cute, fun style.  And the box insert is an example every game publisher should follow.  It properly contains and separates all the components making them easy to organize and retrieve on repeat plays.  The card slots are even designed to hold sleeved cards.

RATING: N/A
Seasons is a card- and dice driven action selection type of game in which each player takes control of a magician that is participating in a grand sorcery tournament held over 12 seasons (hence the name of the game). The winner of this competition is crowned the new archmage.

While all dice are rolled by the same person in the beginning of each round (to establish the available actions that the players choose from), each player have a personal (drafted) deck of cards that is used to form a strategy. 

The goal of the game is to gain the most "magical crystals". During the game, these are gained by card- and dice play, and later on spent as resources in order to play more cards. At the same time, the player with the most crystals at the end of the game, is the winner. As such, it is a strategic game where one must weigh the value of one's existing crystals against the potential gain of spending them on card play.

RATING: 8
Seasons is hard to pin down, but mostly it's a card combination game like, for example, Dominion, Race for the Galaxy and Jambo. Resources come from a common die pool, which adds interesting decisions.

I like it, but even after nearly ten plays, it's still a bit hard to get a feel for the game. Points earned during the game (e.g. by selling energy tokens) seem to matter, but I have the feeling that in most games, the point value of the cards dominate.

But because of the many card sand the drafting system, replayability seems high, so with like-minded players, I can easily see this getting at least a few dozen plays.

RATING: 7.5
The Prelude phase is a very thinky decision-making phase blooming with possibilities and combos that the player needs to carefully navigate through to draft their way to a well-formulated deck of 9. The interactions between card effects are very interesting, and can combo together in clever ways that leave players wanting more. The tournament phase, however, leaves little room for exciting activity save for rolling and choosing dice and can feel like a slow process of harvesting dews from morning leaves in order to fuel the Prelude-designed engine, as the entry of new cards are very limited by dice rolls and availability of energy tokens. Roughly speaking the Prelude, about 10-15% of the game time, can thus determine 80% of the game's outcome. Metaphorically speaking Seasons can feel like a game where, in order to partake in the joy of desigining a car blueprint, the player has to assemble said car with bits and scraps, then also do the work of refilling its fuel tanks and cleaning it up occasionally. Aside, nearing the final year in Seasons causes players to crash into elongated periods of analysis paralysis and optimization bouts, making downtime very painfully long. Last year notwithstanding normal turns can also be plagued by downtime as there is virtually nothing to do on another player's turn save for resolving usually-bad events brought about by their familiars. Gifted, but can be bogged down by potential gameplay fiddliness, downtime, and feelings of mundaneness in the tournament phase especially for players with a fear of the above.

RATING: 8
Strange, but good game.

I say strange, because normally you are looking for resources to buy you (a part of) the card subset of options (think any deckbuilder/dice-builder). Here, you begin with a set of cards, but if you focus on the resources (both energy as summoning power) you'll have about half the game left with no cards to play anymore. And if that happens, it is boring.

Downtime is also an issue, as in the midgame your options explode (which cards to play, in which order), giving a slight 'grinding' feel which is worse the more people are involved. Hence, best with 2, and not recommended at all with 4.

Final (minor) remark: game can be a bit fiddly because the markers that track everything are just small cubes sitting upon smaller score tracks. Don't bump your table.

Now, why is it a good game? As the cards themselves are interesting to play, you can make cool combinations, the component quality is excellent, the choices you make in your turn (if you have anything to do) are interesting and the drafting part (as soon as you know what you are doing) injects a great amount of strategy into the game. 

Requires some plays to get into, as the pre-constructed decks might not be your playstyle, and if you don't know that cards are so valuable you might focus on energy/summoning first only to discover you've played them all well before the end of the game. If you are aware of that and have moved beyond pre-constructed the game begins to show it's greatness. 

A more involved dice driven card game. For fans of fantasy, casting and strategy.

RATING: 5
I don't think I'd play this again with four, I think two is probably the right number to play with.

This seems to be the first game I've seen which was made following the success of 7 Wonders because EVERYTHING is drafted here; draft your cards initially, then draft dice the whole way through the game. Unfortunately what is missing here are simultaneous actions. This game drags. Oh, and the 'draw four cards and play one for free' is incredibly stupid in a game where that might score you between 8 and 30 points.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
The draft of the game is so important that the strategy of the game is heavily comparable to Dominion. In Dominion, you decide your strategy before even the first turn. Similarly, in this game, your draft will heavily dictate if you can compete in the game.
After that, I compare the game to the resource management of Settlers of Catan. For instance, you gain resources nearly every turn based on the roll of the dice. Then, you are deciding what to build towards and what resources you need for this turn or a few turns down the road. 
Top it off with beautiful art and the game is worth more plays than most games these days.

However, the emphasis on the draft means that actually playing the game can be a "waste" of time where the cards drafted have already dictated that one player will have a superior game.
Second, the art is nice, but there is actually no story in the game. The resource management takes high precedence over any interest in cards because there isn't actually a world of characters in the game, just fancy names.
Downtime is a problem. The four player game would surely take longer and create odd swings of points. The two player game will be worth playing because of the speed of the game and the impact between the cards will make sense since you will always impact the game whether you add points or make them lose points.

RATING: 7
This is a really fun, novel game; I like it a great deal! Not everyone agrees, my friends are annoying harsh on it (blah), but I felt like there's a lot to love. 

One really cool thing is the game simulates a contest of magic between wizards to determine the new arch-mage... but it's not a game or war or battle. Your actions instead are displays of power meant to impress... So it's essentially non-violent, which is rad if you're worrying about kids and messages to them. The game mostly works off resource management, timing of card plays, and a draft mechanic... which are all well designed and elegant. Set up and tear down are brief, it doesn't take up excessive space, and is fairly easy to learn and teach. The game doesn't run too long either, which is also nice... 30-45 minutes is very doable. If I had to name one problem? While there's some end of round/game scoring to mix things up, it's pretty easy to figure out, if not look and see, where everyone is in terms of points. The result is that because it's possible to find behind to a point you're doomed to lose, and this makes it all the worse because you can know it.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
This game is like a psychedelic hybrid of 7 Wonders and Magic the Gathering--in a good way. In some ways it feel very like 7 Wonders: at its core is the drafting of cards, play is broken up into three distinct stages, etc.. But a difference you'll noticed right away is that this game allows you to build combos which take advantage of complimentary card functions. As a (reformed) MTG "Johnny" I'm delighted by this.

Also, since this game has the entire draft take place at the very beginning, it makes it easier to plan ahead. You don't know all the cards you'll have during the game, but you know enough to make specific plans about the end game from the very beginning. This also helps new players, who in 7 Wonders are unable to anticipate what they'll need in future ages until they start memorizing the cards. Here they can just look at the cards for the next years to remind themselves what resources will be called for.

On the other hand this mechanic means that unlike 7 Wonders you'll spend a lot of time waiting for others to complete turns, which is unfortunate.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
The card draft basically decides the game. In the game I played one player and I put all the cards in play, and the third player left only one in the hand. The score? 280 / 210 / 180. With basically all the cards being used, it shows that there are cards MUCH better than others and if you weren't luck enough ti get them, well, you will lose.

How to make this game work:
1) Play with 2;
2) Select randomly the cards;
3) Give 1 copy of each card picked for each player;
4) The players than select 9 of them and put the rest, shuffled, aside (to make the draw deck). Now no one can complain much about luck (the shuffling is the luck element for this part);
5) Begin the game.

RATING: 6
Decent but unexceptional. 

Somewhat unusually, it would be much improved by having MORE die rolls. As it is, there are so few that the whole "seasons" aspect is nearly meaningless. The natural impulse is to plan using the seasonal information, e.g. "winter is coming up, so I'm likely to get blue, so I'll play this and this." However, you might get just one roll of the blue dice (two if you're lucky), and there's an excellent chance there will be no blue rolled.

I have no particular objection to having the game be more about using what you get than planning, but it does make the four sets of dice seem silly.

RATING: 7
Good:
-Drafting
-More drafting
-Cool card interactions
-Dice rolling adds variance
-Drafting dice significantly mitigates luck
-Variant for new players
-Simpler to play than it looks
-Deeper in strategy than it looks
-Great dice
-Fantastic artwork
-Well designed inlay
-Great CCG/TCG detox game

Bad:
-Can drag with four players
-Scores bounce up and down too often
-Some cards are needlessly fiddly
-Can be too many permanent effects to track
-Unbalanced cards
-Only 50 unique cards and 100 total
-Playing someone with two of the same permanent effect
-Why crystals and prestige?

Overall:
If you miss or just detoxed from a CCG/TCG, this is great transition game for you.  If you have little to no CCG/TCG/LCG experience and your competition does, prepare to lose the first few games and still have a great time.  I'd recommend that people brand new to board games hold off until they get a little more experience with the hobby.  Also, the game seems to play better after some of the more fiddly cards are removed.  I have elected to remove cards that cause the score to see-saw because they made the scoring too fiddly.

RATING: 8
I know this game already came out at Gencon, but for me it is THE highlight of Essen 2012. It is very addictive and looks absolutely gorgeous when laid out on the table. My girlfriend and I played our first two games with the recommended beginner decks. After those plays, we felt we were familiar with all the beginner cards, and ready to mix in the drafting mechanic. I am sure that this will ensure tons of replayability, even before we touch the advanced cards. So far, I have not experienced any really big gaps in our scoring, but even if that would happen, it will not be so bad because experienced players can finish a game within an hour. Highly recommended.
UPDATE: Played with drafting AND the advanced cards, and loved it even more than before. Not as hard as imagined, great fun, very addictive. The final tally of points is more often than not very surprising!
UPDATE: Finally got a game in with three players. The drafting and the familiar cards get more interesting with more players, but considering the randomness in the game, the longer playing time is a disadvantage. In the end, I like it better with two. Rating settles on an 8.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Cute game, great graphics and a thematically a bit reminiscent of Magic where creatures and items are summoned. The comparison stops right there though. Here, the summoned creatures do nothing except score victory points in the end (oh how so Euro). In any case, the game is quite novel and I haven't quite played anything like it. Elemental magics are harvested by rolling and selecting dice. There is a balancing act between cashing in on the elemental tokens during by transmuting vs. using them to summon creatures or objects. The push pull is nice. The one variable that can be rather swingy are the die rolls. Only a subset of dice are rolled and one is selected each turn. You get what you get. sometimes, the best laid plans are wasted because the dice gods are not in your favor. Fun game. I can see why there is an ardent fan base for this game.

RATING: 1
Seasons is a draft game, with more meat on the bones than 7 Wonders. Its a curious game and I remained intrigued after my first play, enough to give it a second chance. The most recent play left me less inclined to play again since it I drafted quite well, only to have an opponent draw extra cards to steal a win. 

I felt like there wasn't much chance for me to change strategies as the 'Draw a Card' option didn't come up on the dice so I was locked into a losing strategy. I wonder if this game wouldn't benefit from multiple drafts between years only so you have more options to maneuver.

The artwork is gorgeous, just a pity the game isn't as rich as I'd like. Magic the Gathering is still the best draft game in existence, everything just pales in comparison to it.

15/07/14 - Rating dropped from 4 to 2. The taxing strategy seems incredibly dominant, drowning out any other strategy. The game is swingy (with the luck of the draw) and devoid of answers to the tax strategy. I'm done with this game.

15/10/14 - Rating dropped from 2 to 1. Not sure why I didn't just drop this straight to 1. Never playing this again.
